10.1016/j.jmp.2009.01.003
Empirical applications of additive utility theory, when used to analyse preferences in terms of attribute bundles, lead to several problems. Assume, for example, an experimental setup designed to examine customer preferences within Kevin Lancaster’s theory ( Lancaster, 1971 ) where the subject is asked to rank a set of given items. The difficulties arise when the empirical data are to be linked with the mathematical formalism of the theory. Usually, items are represented by product spaces such that a suitable embedding relation of the objects into such a space must be found. The immediate problem which appears is that not all combinations of properties correspond to objects which are physically available or even meaningful in the experimental setup. Mathematically speaking, the mathematical theory must be capable of dealing with subsets of product spaces. The second problem from the standpoint of the experimenter is whether she has chosen the attributes which are relevant to the experimental subject. If a relevant attribute is missing, objects are misrepresented in the factor space in so far as two real-world objects ranked differently by the experimental subject might be accidentally mapped to the same element of the representing space. The question which attributes are relevant to the subject is itself an empirical one and cannot be posed a priori. Besides, arbitrarily extending the factor space might lead to problems of the first type again.

If the strategy is to make the factor space sufficiently rich, a third problem will occur. It would be preferable to have a representation of the empirical items independently of its attributes. While the pure empirical information is captured by preference orders over a set of objects, the set of relative attributes belong to the theory the experimenter is testing, but is not given by the empirical setup. The separation of the empirical from the theoretical level could be achieved by assigning different spaces to them, where the empirical space might be embedded into the theoretical space. Since this could become awkward, attributes should better be represented simply by subsets of the empirical space. Taking a ground space and a set of subsets as the theoretical ingredients would also solve a fourth problem, namely to generalise the whole procedure from ranking objects to ranking of possible (small) worlds, where the subsets represent propositions.

In this paper we analyse a given preference relation in terms of a qualitative representation of a set of desires. To define such a so-called  desire structure , we use an utilitarian representation of conditional desires. We state conditions under which a given preference weak-ordering (complete and transitive) has an additive representation over a given desire structure. These conditions can be effectively computed from the preference relation. The question whether a certain set of desires is sufficient to represent a given preference weak-ordering thus becomes an empirically meaningful and testable statement. The purpose of this paper is to study the link between the utility representation of desires of a given preference weak-ordering, and to state a necessary and sufficient condition under which the latter has an additive representation over the desires, which is then serving as an explanatory foundation of preferences. Our theorems need some restrictions on the desire structure, which are discussed in Section  4  before  Definition 2 .

The paper is organised as follows: Section  2  discusses the related work. In Section  3 , we set some basic definitions of qualitative decision theory and in Section  4 , we analyse the logical structure of a desire structure. Section  5  deals with the non-supervenience condition. This condition says that any desire introduces a partition in the information structure. Section  6  includes a representation theorem which completely characterises the additive representation of a desire structure. Finally Section  7  concludes.

As things stand, the problem of representing desires by additive utility when the space is not a factor space, is intimately linked to the formalism of property spaces. 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         In  Nehring and Puppe (2007, p. 278) , Nehring and Puppe present an additive representation similar to  (4.1)  for the special case of single peaked preferences. 
                       A property space is simply a collection of properties, represented by sets, such that no two elements share the same properties. From property spaces, convex structures are formed by first adding the complements or negations for each property, and second forming the closure under arbitrary intersection. The convex structure determines the geometry of the space, such that it is possible to characterize, for example, attribute factor spaces. The following example demonstrates that our setting is more general than that.

Fig. 1 
                       gives an idea of the relation between properties and geometry. The right-hand side of the figure depicts a space consisting of seven possible worlds, and three propositions  
                         A 
                      , 
                         B 
                      , 
                         C 
                      . These three propositions can be regarded as more or less desirable features. Together, they form a set of sets  
                         Σ 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            A 
                            , 
                            B 
                            , 
                            C 
                            } 
                         
                      , which satisfies property we shall later exploit called  non-supervenience . This is, roughly speaking, to say that none of the three propositions can be defined in terms of the other two. For example, all sets which can be defined from  
                         B 
                       and  
                         C 
                       are unions of some of the sets  
                         
                            { 
                            B 
                            ∩ 
                            C 
                            , 
                            B 
                            ∖ 
                            C 
                            , 
                            C 
                            ∖ 
                            B 
                            , 
                            ∖ 
                            B 
                            ∩ 
                            ∖ 
                            C 
                            } 
                         
                      . No selection of them covers  
                         A 
                       exactly. On the left-hand side of the figure there is the corresponding convex structure. The lines can be understood as follows: Each possible world is between two others if and only if it lies on some shortest path connecting them. The betweenness relation holds if and only if all properties (or negations of them), which the two other worlds have in common, are also shared by the one in between. For example, the uppermost world in the diagram lies in between its two neighbours left and right below, since all properties, which they have in common - in this case only  
                         A 
                      - also hold for the upper world. The convex structure induced by this betweenness-relation is a so-called Quasi-Median space, a space which differs significantly from attribute factor spaces.

Nevertheless, there is still a conceptual mismatch from the psychological point of view. Subsets of a space might not always correspond one-to-one to the motives or desires of the subject. This is due to the conditional nature of desires. A motive or desire is not always effective, but appears and disappears under certain circumstances. Satisfaction and disappointment are both different from a neutral state of absence of desires. These circumstances might include the subject’s expectations: Kahneman and and Tversky have demonstrated the asymmetry of satisfaction and disappointment (loss aversion) within  Kahneman and Tversky (2000)  and outside  Tversky and Kahneman (1991)  the context of decisions under risk. If additive utility theory is intended to explain the desires of a subject, desires have to be represented as conditional desires.

The utilitarian representation of conditional desires, which is tantamount to our approach, has been proposed by  Lang (1996) . It comes with a notion of a dominance preorder of possible worlds similar to our  Definition 5 . Even though for many examples, representation by utility functions are given, a general existence theorem for an additive utility representation is still missing. The utilitarian semantics was first used in the context of Qualitative Decision Theory by  Lang, van der Torre, and Weydert (2002) , but differs from the classical multi-criteria approach (e.g., see  Dubois, Fargier, Perny, and Prade (2001) ) in that the criteria are conditional and they do not separate as product spaces. A framework related to Qualitative Decision Theory was later independently applied to an entirely different problem by  Schoch (2000) , where it was used to formulate a cardinal measure of explanatory coherence over possible worlds, which decomposes additively over explanatory relations between propositions. These explanatory relations are being treated like conditional desires, with successful explanations contributing a positive term to the coherence function, while explanatory anomalies result in a negative punishment term.

Utilitarian representations of goals have been proposed for a certain time (e.g.  Doyle and Wellman (1991)  and  Bacchus and Grove (1997) ), but they stick to the paradigm of attribute factorisations which we have just criticised. Typically for that approach, in  Doyle and Wellman (1991)  (for an update see  Doyle and McGeachie (2004) ), goals are derived from a preference order using an embedding relation into a factor space, such that they fulfill our strong monotonicity condition from  Definition 6 . We believe that, in general, desires or goals cannot be identified with elementary attributes in product spaces. Many desires are conditional and thus depend on the fulfillment of others. Consider, for example, the two desires ‘I would like to eat potatoes now’ and ‘If I am being served potatoes I strongly prefer them cooked (otherwise I prefer not to eat them at all and have something else).’ With respect to their product space, the two attributes  
                         
                            { 
                            potatoes 
                            , 
                            ¬ 
                            potatoes 
                            } 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            { 
                            cooked 
                            , 
                            ¬ 
                            cooked 
                            } 
                         
                       are not  preferentially independent  in the sense of  Keeney and Raiffa (1976) : The preference for potatoes is reversed under the condition they are being served raw. The preferential entanglement of attributes reflects the  prima facie  character of desires. In contrast to the unconditional nature of the agent’s preference for potatoes, there is no ceteris paribus condition which allows us to extract the desire from their preference structure. The alleged solution ( Doyle & Wellman, 1991 , p. 701) of choosing logically dependent attributes seems alluring, but we believe it will not work out with more complicated examples, especially when multi-valued attributes are involved.

The model of  Lang et al. (2002) , on the other hand, allows to represent conditional desires by propositions of any logical form and in arbitrary combinations. We take from it the basic notion of desires and their representation by local utility functions, as well as the dominance ordering. The additive approach to utility representation of the desire structure is also adopted. We remain, however, completely within the framework of utility theory and do not attempt to specify a utilitarian semantics.

The core of our model is to give an additive utility representation of the preferences, such that total utility is considered the sum of all utilities stemming from desire fulfillments. In the discussion below, however, we will defend only a weaker condition called separability. Although separability is only a necessary condition for additivity, it is reasonable to achieve additivity. The step from separability to additivity is small and common, and fortunately, we are in a position to prove the existence of such a representation for a wide class of desire structures. Separable and non-additive functions are hard to construct and quite exotic, and we are not aware of any application. Examples of a separable non-additive functions could be found in  Fishburn (1970, p. 43) .

The notation in this section on desires and their associated utility functions is a simplified version borrowed from  Lang et al. (2002) , which in turn generalises  Lang (1996)  to the three sorts of desires outlined below. Here, we do not refer to the semantics of conditional desires, which is slightly different in both papers. Let  
                         W 
                       be a set of possible worlds. We assign to it an algebra  
                         L 
                       of subsets of  
                         W 
                       representing the propositions, or the language, in which the content and the condition of the desire could be formulated. Alternatively,  
                         W 
                       could also stand for a set of individuals, where  
                         L 
                       be the algebra generated by their properties. Within the model, we can differentiate between three types of desires.

Loss desires 
                         
                            A loss desire stands for the agent’s preference to avoid a certain circumstance. An example for a loss desire is “I don’t mind the color of my car, unless it is black, which I hate.” 
                         
                         Win desires 
                         
                            A win desire expresses the agent’s positive acknowledgement of an achievement without regret in its absence. The wish “If I eat fish I would like to have white Burgundy wine with it (but I don’t mind if wine is not available and I have just water)” represents a pure win desire. 
                         
                         Mixed desires 
                         
                            Some desires combine both aspects. The above example “If I eat potatoes, I would like to have them cooked (I like potatoes, but I hate to be served raw ones)” is a mixed desire.

A loss desire stands for the agent’s preference to avoid a certain circumstance. An example for a loss desire is “I don’t mind the color of my car, unless it is black, which I hate.”

A win desire expresses the agent’s positive acknowledgement of an achievement without regret in its absence. The wish “If I eat fish I would like to have white Burgundy wine with it (but I don’t mind if wine is not available and I have just water)” represents a pure win desire.

Some desires combine both aspects. The above example “If I eat potatoes, I would like to have them cooked (I like potatoes, but I hate to be served raw ones)” is a mixed desire.

The reason why we have chosen an additive aggregation of desires is twofold. The first reason is a pragmatic one. Additive functions of type  (3.2)  are easily characterised, and have desirable mathematical features, especially in statistical contexts or in the presence of risk. The second reason regards the concept of desires and is the most substantial argument. Additive aggregations satisfy the principle of separability with respect to the set of desires. Separability keeps the different desires semantically independent of each other in the following sense: Keep one desire fixed and restrict the preference relation induced by  (3.2)  to a maximal subset of elements of  
                         W 
                       which have the same local utility for this desire (there are either two or three such subspaces for each desire according to its type). These restricted preference relations coincide in the sense that they agree if those elements of  
                         W 
                       are identified which differ only with respect to the chosen fixed desires. If separability is violated, desires are interdependent in a way not expressed by the conditional nature of desires. That is to say, the ‘logic’ of desires is not captured by their qualitative structure. Even though separability is only a necessary though not a sufficient condition for additivity, additive aggregation functions are by far the best way to ensure separability. Observe also that separability ensures that a degenerated desire with  
                         
                            
                               A 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         
                            
                               B 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                         = 
                         W 
                       cancels out of the aggregation function: Its local utility function contributes to the total sum only by adding a (non-negative) constant preserving the preference order.

The local utility function of each mixed desire can be written as a sum of local utility functions of a win and a loss desire. We call win and loss desires  elementary desires , which are sufficient by themselves, since the representation  (3.2)  can always be decomposed into win and loss desires. The qualitative structure of desires can therefore best be represented by dividing the set  
                         Δ 
                       of desires into two non-disjoint subsets, the  positive desires

Our approach differs from  Lang et al. (2002)  insofar as we do not attempt to establish a utilitarian semantics, where utility functions or preference orders take the role of semantic models. Lang et al. have proposed that a  single  desire  
                         δ 
                         = 
                         
                            〈 
                            A 
                            , 
                            B 
                            〉 
                         
                       is modelled by a preference order  
                         ≿ 
                      on  
                         W 
                       iff at least one  
                         A 
                         ∩ 
                         B 
                      -world is (weakly or strongly) preferred over all  
                         B 
                         ∖ 
                         A 
                      -worlds. This criterion seems to be adequate only  ceteris paribus  under the condition that positive and negative desires do not interfere with each other. Assuming a representation of the form  (3.2) , it is easy to see that in the context of other desires overlapping with either  
                         A 
                         ∩ 
                         B 
                       or  
                         B 
                         ∖ 
                         A 
                      , this relation can be actually reversed, such that then the maximal  
                         B 
                         ∖ 
                         A 
                      -world is strongly preferred over all  
                         A 
                         ∩ 
                         B 
                      -worlds. This amounts to the classical situation of a contextual preference reversal. From a semantic standpoint, the original desire  
                         δ 
                       has now become obscured by some others. As will be expected by this insight, the relation between monotonicity (our  Definition 6 ) and semantic representation found in  Lang et al. (2002, proposition 1) , is restricted to the two cases of either pure win or pure loss desires. In contrast to utilitarian semantics, we will adopt a holistic view stating that only a  complete  set  
                         Δ 
                       is representable by the preference order, which holds if and only if its utility function can be written in the form  (3.2) . It must remain an open problem here, however, if for a desire structure satisfying our premise given by  Definition 9 , utilitarian semantics is compatible with our approach in the sense that whenever a utility function represents the desire structure in our sense, all desires are modelled by the utility function in the semantic sense.

There is a major conceptual difference between norms and desires. In deontic logic it is normally not assumed that for the dyadic deontic operator  
                         O 
                         
                            ( 
                            A 
                            | 
                            B 
                            ) 
                         
                       representing a  norm  
                      
                         A 
                       conditional on  
                         B 
                       we must have  
                         A 
                         ∧ 
                         B 
                       consistent. We might insist that, even if the violation of a conditional obligation has occurred, the norm would still hold under this condition. For example, in a case of murder, even if the crime has irreversibly been committed, the norm not to do so would still be assumed to hold. The difference in logic between norms and desires is that in the corresponding (Kripkean) semantic of norms real worlds are linked to morally feasible worlds, while the logic of desires represents a decision theoretical concept. In contrast to norms, desires share with goals the principle of fulfillment expressed by the condition below,  
                         
                            (4.2) 
                            
                               A 
                               ⊆ 
                               B 
                               , 
                            
                         
                       while a conditional norm of the type ‘obligations toward  
                         A 
                      , given  
                         ¬ 
                         A 
                      ’ would still make sense. As emphasised earlier, substituting  
                         A 
                       by  
                         A 
                         ∩ 
                         B 
                       leaves local utility functions unchanged. Thus, condition  (4.2)  does not impose any restriction on ordinal (or even cardinal) structures assigned to desires.

                      consistent  if and only if

A major difference between a pure qualitative approach on the one hand, and desires or goals on the other is the subsumption of logically weaker goals or norms under stronger ones. Assume a biconditional

                               B

                               O 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  . 
                                  | 
                                  . 
                                  ) 
                               
                             is extensional in both arguments, allowing for substitution of equivalent propositions. 
                       If the monotonicity property holds, it follows that within a purely qualitative context, a weaker norm like ‘you should not kill’ is therefore reducible to the stronger norm ‘you should not injure’ whenever killing implies injuring the victim. Therefore, some dyadic deontic logics have dropped the monotonicity condition. This, however, could get conceptually quite complicated. In a quantitative context, this problem is elegantly solved. Two norms or goals can be logically depended, but are clearly differentiated. If strengths of norm violations were to be introduced, an injury is definitely of lesser severity than a murder. The same holds for quantitative conditional desires, where the desire ‘I strongly prefer a Mercedes car’ is to be differentiated from ‘I would be happy to have a car at all’.

Conditions (i) and (ii) of the definition of a desire structure ( Definition 2 ) ensure that each desire structure is associated with exactly one family of characteristic sets. Clause (iii) ensures that each characteristic set can be associated with either a positive or a negative desire.

                                     0̸

The following section introduces the concept of supervenience needed to impose restrictions on the set-theoretical structure of desires. A property or a proposition  
                         S 
                       is said to supervene on a set  
                         Σ 
                       of properties or propositions if and only if, from a complete information on  
                         Σ 
                      , it is determined whether  
                         S 
                       holds or not. In other words, it is impossible for there to be two individuals or possible worlds to be alike in respect to each property in  
                         Σ 
                       without being alike in respect to  
                         S 
                      . For the case of individuals, this is, in the philosophical literature, often called weak or intraworld supervenience ( Humberstone, 1998 ). If the sets involved contain possible worlds, our notion of supervenience corresponds to Padoa’s criterion of implicit definability (see  Appendix A ). Since we leave the interpretation of  
                         W 
                       open, we prefer the more general term of supervenience.

We will assume that a feasible set of desires is independent in the sense that no supervenience relation holds between the desires: Any desire introduces a new partition in the information structure generated by the characteristic sets. In other words, for each desire  
                         δ 
                       there is a set  
                         C 
                         ⊆ 
                         W 
                       on which, in the sense of a  ceteris paribus condition , all other desires are equally satisfied, and which is subdivided by the characteristic set of the desire into two non-empty subsets. This seems to be a natural condition, which also allows for hierarchically ordered desires, as in the potato example above.

Assume an agent has a preference order that is representable by a desire structure with characteristic sets  
                         Σ 
                      . Then the following equivalence relation represents the  information structure  on  
                         W 
                       given by their desires:

Supervenience is intimately linked to several concepts of logical definability. The relation between logical definability and our purely set-theoretical terminology shall be explained in more detail in  Appendix A . However, since these details are not essential for further argumentation, they can be skipped by readers not interested in (or not familiar with) logic.

We now impose a non-supervenience condition on desire structures which will be a prerequisite for our representation theorem and for the relative uniqueness of the representation. Broadly speaking, this condition states that there should be no redundancy in the information structure. This relaxes the attribute factorisability conditions of  Doyle and McGeachie (2004) . Non-supervenience is trivially satisfied when  
                         W 
                       forms a product space and  
                         Σ 
                       contains a maximally independent set of elementary propositions.

                               Σ

                                     Σ 
                                     .

                               D 
                             is called  non-supervenient  iff its characteristic sets  
                               Σ 
                             are non-supervenient.

It is easy to see that  
                         
                            
                               Λ 
                            
                            
                               p 
                            
                         
                       is an  equivalence relation . In order to achieve an additive representation, we introduce the cancellation conditions  
                         
                            
                               
                                  C 
                               
                            
                            
                               p 
                            
                         
                       by  Tversky (1964)  and  Fishburn (1970)  (See also  Fishburn (2001)  or  Wakker (1989) ). They roughly state that if two vectors, as those above, only differ by the distribution of the properties, one vector from  
                         
                            
                               W 
                            
                            
                               p 
                            
                         
                       cannot dominate the other in a strict sense analogous to the strong Pareto principle. That is to say, if  
                         
                            
                               ω 
                            
                            
                               k 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                         ≿ 
                         
                            
                               ω 
                            
                            
                               k 
                            
                         
                       holds for all  
                         k 
                         = 
                         1 
                       to  
                         p 
                      , then none of these relations can be strict. This is quite a natural assumption, since if one vector is strictly “better than” the other, and the characteristic sets from  
                         Σ 
                       are the only relevant ones, then there must be a difference with respect to that set. The condition states that the distribution of the properties cannot be relevant. Otherwise, the desires would be dependent on each other in a way not described by the conditional structure of the desires. But the dependence of desires should only be captured by its conditional structure. Since the relation  
                         
                            (

                                  1 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            … 
                            ,

                            …

                                  ω 
                               
                               
                                  p 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       is invariant under arbitrary and independent permutation of both vectors, it is sufficient to state the non-dominance (or cancellation) conditions with respect to the rightmost elements  
                         
                            
                               ω 
                            
                            
                               p 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               ω 
                            
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                       of the two vectors. The cancellation conditions thus read

Remark 2 
                         If Conditions C 2  and C 3  are satisfied then

If Conditions C 2  and C 3  are satisfied then  
                               ≿ 
                             is a weak order.

Cancellation conditions (C 2 ,C 3 ,…) are well known in additive measurement theory. From a theoretical point of view, they are necessary conditions for representability by an additive utility function. In order to understand these conditions, let us take the case of C 2 . It states that if  
                         
                            (

                            (

                                  ω 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       with respect to each “property”  
                         S 
                         ∈ 
                         Σ 
                      , and  
                         
                            
                               ω 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                         ≿ 
                         
                            
                               ω 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       then it must be the case that  
                         n 
                         o

                      , the cancellation conditions can be easily and effectively computed.

                            , the coefficients  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     γ 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                  
                               
                             
                            are uniquely defined, and there are always more constraints on  
                            
                               U 
                            
                            than needed to determine the coefficients.

Broadly speaking, this theorem states the following: Suppose that there is no redundancy in the information structure of an agent ( 
                         D 
                         = 
                         
                            〈 
                            
                               
                                  Δ 
                               
                               
                                  + 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  Δ 
                               
                               
                                  − 
                               
                            
                            〉 
                         
                       is a non-supervenient desire structure), then his preference relation  
                         ≿ 
                       over  
                         W 
                       (the set of possible words) is representable by an additive utility function whose coefficients are in accordance with his desire structure  
                         D 
                       if and only if the cancellation conditions are fulfilled and the preference relation  
                         ≿ 
                       satisfies a monotonicity condition. The main purpose of this theorem is to show that it is possible to construct a desire-based utility function representing a preference relation. This theorem could be seen as a meta-result for constructing psychological theories. Indeed, it helps to understand the motives of the subject behind his preference. For instance, in our theory, although two subjects might have different preference relations, which may be representable by the same desire structure, these two subjects differ in the quantitative weights of their motives, though agreeing on the qualitative structures of their motives. Likewise, in our theory two subjects may disagree on the qualitative structures of their motives (two different desire structures), though possessing the same quantitative weights of these motives (same utility). Let us recall that this latter situation often arises in game theory.

The uniqueness property is especially useful in case  
                         ≿ 
                       stems from a richer framework ensuring cardinal utilities, which is unique up to a positive affine transformation. In this case the coefficients are unique up to a common positive factor. The last statement is meant to show that under the condition of non-supervenience, desire theory has an empirical content: For a given (weak order) preference  
                         ≿ 
                       on  
                         W 
                       and a given non-supervenient desire structure  
                         D 
                      , the question whether  
                         ≿ 
                       is  
                         D 
                      -representable is non-trivial.

The formalism of Qualitative Decision Theory has been designed to model goals for automatic decisions. It is, however, also suitable for analysing preferences in terms of the motives of the agent, which we have called his or her desires. We have so far provided a general tool for the quantitative analysis of preferences in terms of desires. The formalism could be taken, for example, to investigate whether customer preferences are related to the properties of the goods the consumer favors, but unlike Kevin Lancaster’s ( Lancaster, 1971 ) concept of goods as bundles of attributes, we do not restrict ourselves to factor spaces. We only assume that none of the properties in question supervenes on the others, but we do not require that all logically possible combinations of properties correspond to a good. Desires for goods are conflicting, and we hardly expect a dominant good to exist.

We wish also to thank Jérôme Lang, Christophe Gonzales, the participants of the Social Choice and Welfare Conference held in Osaka (July 2004) and of seminars held at the University of Paris I Panthéon-Sorbonne and the University of Evry for their comments.

This paper was written while Daniel SCHOCH was visiting at the University of Evry. Major revisions were made during his stay at the Centre d’Études de l’Emploi (CEE), Noisy-Le-Grand. The authors owe thanks to both organisations for their generous financial support.
